Although the stores have been called "stealth Starbucks"<sup id="cite_ref-stealth_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stealth-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Simon_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simon-184">[184]</a></sup> and criticized as "local-washing,"<sup id="cite_ref-Eaves_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eaves-185">[185]</a></sup> Schultz says that "It wasn't so much that we were trying to hide the brand, but trying to do things in those stores that we did not feel were appropriate for Starbucks."<sup id="cite_ref-marketing_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marketing-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Licensed_and_franchise_operations">Licensed and franchise operations</span></h3>
<p>Independently operated Starbucks locations exist. Stores that independently operate locations include <a href="/wiki/Ahold_Delhaize" title="Ahold Delhaize">Ahold Delhaize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barnes_%26_Noble" title="Barnes &amp; Noble">Barnes &amp; Noble</a>, <a href="/wiki/Target_Corporation" title="Target Corporation">Target</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albertsons" title="Albertsons">Albertsons</a> and, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Publix" title="Publix">Publix</a> stores. As of 2015, 4,962 licensed locations exist.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/EMEA" class="mw-redirect" title="EMEA">EMEA</a> (Europe, Middle East, and Africa) markets, Starbucks operates a franchising program. Different from the licensing program in which existing corporations may apply to operate a Starbucks kiosk within an existing store, franchises can enable new, freestanding stores.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Automated_locations">Automated locations</span></h3>
<p>Starbucks has <a href="/wiki/Automation" title="Automation">automated</a> systems in some areas. These machines have 280 possible drink combinations to choose from. They have touchscreens and customers can play games while they wait for their order.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Facilities">Facilities</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_Coffee_Dortmund_024.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Starbucks_Coffee_Dortmund_024.jpg/220px-Starbucks_Coffee_Dortmund_024.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Starbucks_Coffee_Dortmund_024.jpg/220px-Starbucks_Coffee_Dortmund_024.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_Coffee_Dortmund_024.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Starbucks in <a href="/wiki/Dortmund" title="Dortmund">Dortmund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Forum_Bornova02.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Forum_Bornova02.jpg/220px-Forum_Bornova02.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Forum_Bornova02.jpg/220px-Forum_Bornova02.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Forum_Bornova02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Starbucks in <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0zmir" title="İzmir">İzmir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a></div></div></div>
<p>Free Wi-Fi Internet access varies in different regions. In Germany, customers get unlimited free Wi-Fi through <a href="/wiki/BT_Openzone" class="mw-redirect" title="BT Openzone">BT Openzone</a>, and in Switzerland and Austria, customers can get 30 minutes with a voucher card (through <a href="/wiki/T-Mobile" title="T-Mobile">T-Mobile</a>).
</p><p>Since 2003, Starbucks in the UK rolled out a paid Wi-Fi based on one-time, hourly or daily payment. Then, in September 2009, it was changed to 100% free Wi-Fi at most of its outlets. Customers with a Starbucks Card are able to log-on to the Wi-Fi in-store for free with their card details, thereby bringing the benefits of the loyalty program in line with the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Since July 2010, Starbucks has offered free Wi-Fi in all of its US stores via AT&amp;T and information through a partnership with <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a>. This is an effort to be more competitive against local chains, which have long offered free Wi-Fi, and against <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a>, which began offering free wireless internet access in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> On June 30, 2010, Starbucks announced it would begin to offer unlimited and free Internet access via Wi-Fi to customers in all company-owned locations across Canada starting on July 1, 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p><p>In October 2012, Starbucks and Duracell <a href="/wiki/Powermat" class="mw-redirect" title="Powermat">Powermat</a> announced a pilot program to install Powermat charging surfaces in the tabletops in selected Starbucks stores in the Boston area.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Furthermore, Starbucks announced its support in the <a href="/wiki/Power_Matters_Alliance" title="Power Matters Alliance">Power Matters Alliance</a> (PMA) and its membership in the PMA board, along with Google and AT&amp;T, in an effort to create "a real-world ecosystem of wireless power" through a universal wireless charging standard that customers could use to recharge smartphones.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising">Advertising</span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Logo">Logo</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption-center{text-align:center;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:129px;max-width:129px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Sbux_logo_pre_1987.gif" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e9/Sbux_logo_pre_1987.gif/125px-Sbux_logo_pre_1987.gif" decoding="async" width="125" height="125" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="200"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 125px;height: 125px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e9/Sbux_logo_pre_1987.gif/125px-Sbux_logo_pre_1987.gif" data-alt="" data-width="125" data-height="125"> </span></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">1971–1987</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_Coffee_logo_1987.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Starbucks_Coffee_logo_1987.png/125px-Starbucks_Coffee_logo_1987.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="124" data-file-width="224" data-file-height="222"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 125px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4f/Starbucks_Coffee_logo_1987.png/125px-Starbucks_Coffee_logo_1987.png" data-alt="" data-width="125" data-height="124"> </span></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">1987–1992</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_Coffee_Logo.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/Starbucks_Coffee_Logo.svg/125px-Starbucks_Coffee_Logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="125" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="200"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 125px;height: 125px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/Starbucks_Coffee_Logo.svg/125px-Starbucks_Coffee_Logo.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="125" data-height="125" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/Starbucks_Coffee_Logo.svg/188px-Starbucks_Coffee_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/Starbucks_Coffee_Logo.svg/250px-Starbucks_Coffee_Logo.svg.png 2x"> </span></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">1992–2011</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_Corporation_Logo_2011.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/Starbucks_Corporation_Logo_2011.svg/125px-Starbucks_Corporation_Logo_2011.svg.png" decoding="async" width="125" height="127" data-file-width="237" data-file-height="240"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 125px;height: 127px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/Starbucks_Corporation_Logo_2011.svg/125px-Starbucks_Corporation_Logo_2011.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="125" data-height="127" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/Starbucks_Corporation_Logo_2011.svg/188px-Starbucks_Corporation_Logo_2011.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/Starbucks_Corporation_Logo_2011.svg/250px-Starbucks_Corporation_Logo_2011.svg.png 2x"> </span></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">2011–present</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In 2006, Valerie O'Neil, a Starbucks spokeswoman, said that the logo is an image of a "twin-tailed mermaid, or <a href="/wiki/Siren_(mythology)" title="Siren (mythology)">siren</a> as she's known in Greek mythology."<sup id="cite_ref-pi-logo_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pi-logo-193">[193]</a></sup> The logo has been significantly streamlined over the years. In the first version,<sup id="cite_ref-pouryourheart_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pouryourheart-194">[194]</a></sup> the Starbucks siren was topless and had a fully visible double fish tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Pren253_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pren253-195">[195]</a></sup> The image also had a rough visual texture and has been likened to a <a href="/wiki/Melusine" title="Melusine">melusine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> The image is said by Starbucks to be based on a 16th-century "<a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Norse</a>" <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodcut</a>, although other scholars note that it is apparently based on a 15th-century woodcut in <a href="/wiki/Juan_Eduardo_Cirlot" title="Juan Eduardo Cirlot">J.E. Cirlot's</a> <i>Dictionary of Symbols</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup></p><p>In the second version, which was used from 1987–92, her breasts were covered by her flowing hair, but her navel was still visible.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> The fish tail was cropped slightly, and the primary color was changed from brown to green, a nod to the Alma Mater of the three founders, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_San_Francisco" title="University of San Francisco">University of San Francisco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> In the third version, used between 1992 and 2011, her navel and breasts are not visible at all, and only vestiges remain of the fish tails. The original "woodcut" logo has been moved to the Starbucks's Headquarters in Seattle.
</p><p>At the beginning of September 2006 and then again in early 2008, Starbucks temporarily reintroduced its original brown logo on paper hot-drink cups. Starbucks has stated that this was done to show the company's heritage from the Pacific Northwest and to celebrate 35 years of business. The vintage logo sparked some controversy due in part to the siren's bare breasts,<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> but the temporary switch garnered little attention from the media. Starbucks had drawn similar criticism when they reintroduced the vintage logo in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> The logo was altered when Starbucks entered the Saudi Arabian market in 2000 to remove the siren, leaving only her crown,<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> as reported in a <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>-winning column by <a href="/wiki/Colbert_I._King" title="Colbert I. King">Colbert I. King</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> in 2002. The company announced three months later that it would be using the international logo in Saudi Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2011, Starbucks announced that they would make small changes to the company's logo, removing the Starbucks wordmark around the siren, enlarging the siren image, and making it green.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Partnerships">Partnerships</span></h3>
<p>Starbucks has agreed to a partnership with <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a> to collaborate on selling music as part of the "coffeehouse experience." In October 2006, Apple added a Starbucks Entertainment area to the <a href="/wiki/ITunes_Store" title="ITunes Store">iTunes Store</a>, selling music similar to that played in Starbucks stores. In September 2007, Apple announced that customers would be able to browse the iTunes Store at Starbucks via Wi-Fi in the US—with no requirement to log into the Wi-Fi network—targeted at <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a>, <a href="/wiki/IPod_touch" class="mw-redirect" title="IPod touch">iPod touch</a>, <a href="/wiki/IPad" title="IPad">iPad</a>, and <a href="/wiki/MacBook" title="MacBook">MacBook</a> users. The iTunes Store will automatically detect recent songs playing in a Starbucks and offer users the opportunity to download the tracks. Some stores feature LCD screens with the artist name, song, and album information of the current song playing. This feature has been rolled out in <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>, New York City, and the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay_Area" title="San Francisco Bay Area">San Francisco Bay Area</a>, and was offered in limited markets during 2007–2008.<sup id="cite_ref-informationweek_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-informationweek-207">[207]</a></sup> During the fall of 2007, Starbucks also began to sell digital downloads of certain albums through iTunes. Starbucks gave away 37 different songs for free download through iTunes as part of the "Song of the Day" promotion in 2007, and a "Pick of the Week" card is now available at Starbucks for a free song download. Since 2011, Starbucks also gives away a "Pick of the Week" card for app downloads from the App Store. A Starbucks app is available in the iPhone <a href="/wiki/App_Store_(iOS)" title="App Store (iOS)">App Store</a>.
</p><p>Starting on June 1, 2009, the <a href="/wiki/MSNBC" title="MSNBC">MSNBC</a> morning news program <i><a href="/wiki/Morning_Joe" title="Morning Joe">Morning Joe</a></i> has been presented as "brewed by Starbucks" and the show's logo changed to include the company logo. Although the hosts have previously consumed Starbucks coffee on air "for free" in the words of MSNBC president <a href="/wiki/Phil_Griffin_(presenter)" class="mw-redirect" title="Phil Griffin (presenter)">Phil Griffin</a>, it was not paid placement at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> The move was met with mixed reactions from rival news organizations, viewed as both a clever partnership in an economic downturn and a compromise of journalistic standards.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></p><p>Starbucks and <a href="/wiki/Kraft_Foods" title="Kraft Foods">Kraft Foods</a> entered into a partnership in 1998 to sell Starbucks products in the Mondelez grocery stores owned by the latter. Starbucks claimed that Kraft did not sufficiently promote its products and offered Kraft US$750 million to terminate the agreement; however, Kraft declined the offer, but Starbucks proceeded with the termination anyway. Starbucks wanted to terminate the agreement because at the time, single coffee packs were beginning to become popular. In their agreement, Starbucks was confined to selling packs that only worked in Kraft's Tassimo machines. Starbucks didn't want to fall behind in the market opportunities for <a href="/wiki/K-Cups" class="mw-redirect" title="K-Cups">k cups</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> In mid-November 2013, an arbitrator ordered Starbucks to pay a fine of US$2.8 billion to Kraft <a href="/wiki/Corporate_spin-off" title="Corporate spin-off">spin-off</a> Mondelez International for its premature unilateral termination of the agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj20131113_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj20131113-213">[213]</a></sup></p><p>In June 2014, Starbucks announced a new partnership with <a href="/wiki/Arizona_State_University" title="Arizona State University">Arizona State University</a> (ASU) that would allow Starbucks employees in their Junior and Senior years of college to complete four years of college at Arizona State University's online program for only around 23K. Starbucks employees admitted into the program will receive a scholarship from the college, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.starbucks.com/careers/college-plan">College Achievement Plan</a> (CAP), that will cover 44% of their tuition. The remaining balance and all other expenses would be paid by the student or through traditional financial aid. In April 2015, Starbucks and ASU announced an expansion of the College Achievement Program. The program would now allow all eligible part-time and full-time employees working in a U.S. Starbucks to enroll in the program for full-tuition reimbursement.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> After the completion of each semester, Starbucks reimburses the student their portion of the tuition. The student can then use the reimbursement to pay any loans or debt incurred during the semester.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup></p><p>In 2015, Starbucks signed a deal with <a href="/wiki/PepsiCo" title="PepsiCo">PepsiCo</a> to market and distribute Starbucks products in several Latin American countries for 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2015, Starbucks entered a partnership with music streaming service <a href="/wiki/Spotify" title="Spotify">Spotify</a>. The partnership entailed giving U.S.-based employees a Spotify premium subscription and to help influence the music played in store via playlists made using Spotify. Starbucks was also given its own curated Spotify playlist to be featured on Spotify's mobile app.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2020, Starbucks announced that starting from April 6, their employees could use up to 2 free mental health therapy sessions for themselves or their family members, like a partner/spouse or children. They can meet with a counselor face-to-face or video call and will also have unlimited access to self-care apps through Lyra Health Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(6)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Parodies_and_infringements">Parodies and infringements</span></h2><section class="mf-section-6 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-6">
<p>Starbucks has been a target of parodies and imitations of its logo, particularly the 1992 version, and has used legal action against those it perceives to be infringing its intellectual property. In 2000, San Francisco <a href="/wiki/Cartoonist" title="Cartoonist">cartoonist</a> <a href="/wiki/Kieron_Dwyer" title="Kieron Dwyer">Kieron Dwyer</a> was sued by Starbucks for copyright and trademark infringement after creating a parody of its siren logo and putting it on the cover of one of his comics, later placing it on coffee mugs, T-shirts, and stickers that he sold on his website and at comic book conventions. Dwyer felt that since his work was a parody it was protected by his right to <a href="/wiki/Free_speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Free speech">free speech</a> under U.S. law. The case was eventually settled <a href="/wiki/Settlement_(litigation)" title="Settlement (litigation)">out of court</a>, as Dwyer claimed he did not have the financial ability to endure a trial case with Starbucks. The judge agreed that Dwyer's work was a parody and thus enjoyed constitutional protection; however, he was forbidden from financially "profiting" from using a "confusingly similar" image of the Starbucks siren logo. Dwyer was allowed to display the image as an expression of free speech, but he can no longer sell it.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> In a similar case, a New York store selling stickers and T-shirts using the Starbucks logo with the phrase "Fuck Off" was sued by the company in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> An anti-Starbucks website, starbuckscoffee.co.uk, which encouraged people to deface the Starbucks logo<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> was transferred to Starbucks in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> but has since resurfaced at www.starbuckscoffee.org.uk. Christian bookstores and websites in the US are selling a T-shirt featuring a logo with the siren replaced by Jesus and the words "Sacrificed for me" around the edge.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup></p><p>Other successful cases filed by Starbucks include the case won in 2006 against the chain Xingbake in Shanghai, China for <a href="/wiki/Trademark_infringement" title="Trademark infringement">trademark infringement</a>, because the chain used a green-and-white circular logo with a name that sounded phonetically similar to the Chinese for Starbucks.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> Starbucks did not open any stores after first registering its trademark in Russia in 1997, and in 2002 a Russian lawyer successfully filed a request to cancel the trademark. He then registered the name with a Moscow company and asked for $600,000 to sell the trademark to Starbucks, but was ruled against in November 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-54">[54]</a></sup></p><p>In 2003, Starbucks sent a cease-and-desist letter to "HaidaBucks Coffee House" in <a href="/wiki/Masset,_British_Columbia" class="mw-redirect" title="Masset, British Columbia">Masset, British Columbia</a>, Canada. The store was owned by a group of young <a href="/wiki/Haida_people" title="Haida people">Haida</a> men, who claimed that the name was a coincidence, due to "buck" being a Haida word for "young man" (a claim that cannot be substantiated). After facing criticism, Starbucks dropped its demand after HaidaBucks dropped "coffee house" from its name.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup></p><p>In the 2004 <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks Animation</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Shrek_2" title="Shrek 2">Shrek 2</a></i>, Starbucks is parodied as Farbucks in the kingdom of Far Far Away, which in turn, is a parody of a medieval version of <a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood">Hollywood</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.
</p><p>Sam Buck Lundberg, who owns a coffee store in Oregon, was prohibited from using "Sambuck's Coffee" on the shop front in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> Starbucks lost a trademark infringement case against a smaller coffee vendor in South Korea that operates coffee stations under the name Starpreya. The company, Elpreya, says Starpreya is named after the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Germanic_deities_and_heroes" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Germanic deities and heroes">Norse goddess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freyja" title="Freyja">Freja</a>, with the letters of that name changed to ease pronunciation by Koreans. The court rejected Starbucks's claim that the logo of Starpreya is too similar to their own logo.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> A bar owner in Galveston, Texas, USA<sup id="cite_ref-StarBockcase_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarBockcase-230">[230]</a></sup> won the right<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to sell "Star Bock Beer" after a lawsuit by Starbucks in 2003 after he registered the name,<sup id="cite_ref-StarBockcase_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarBockcase-230">[230]</a></sup> but the 2005 federal court ruling also stated that the sale of the beer must be restricted to Galveston, a ruling upheld by the Supreme Court in 2007.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>Ongoing cases include a dispute over the copyright application for Seattle's <a href="/wiki/Rat_City_Rollergirls" class="mw-redirect" title="Rat City Rollergirls">Rat City Rollergirls</a> logo in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> The company claimed the roller derby league's logo by a Washington artist<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> was too similar to its own. Starbucks requested an extension to further examine the issue and possibly issue a complaint, which was granted by the Trademark Office. The July 16, 2008, deadline passed without action by the corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p><p>Starbucks launched action against an Indian cosmetics business run by Shahnaz Husain, after she applied to register the name Starstruck for coffee and related products. She said she aimed to open a chain of stores that would sell coffee and chocolate-based cosmetics.<sup id="cite_ref-struck_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-struck-234">[234]</a></sup> A cafe in <a href="/wiki/Al-Manara_Square" title="Al-Manara Square">Al-Manara Square</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Loungo_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Loungo-235">[235]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ramallah" title="Ramallah">Ramallah</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestinian Territories</a>, opened in 2009 with the name "Stars and Bucks" and a logo using a similar green circle and block lettering.<sup id="cite_ref-Stars_and_Bucks_pi_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stars_and_Bucks_pi-236">[236]</a></sup> Like Starbucks, the Stars and Bucks serves cappuccinos in ceramic cups, and offers free Wifi. According to speculation cited in the <i><a href="/wiki/Seattle_Post_Intelligencer" class="mw-redirect" title="Seattle Post Intelligencer">Seattle Post Intelligencer</a></i>, the cafe's name and imitation Starbucks style may be a political satire of American consumerism. Starbucks is not known to have taken action against this business.
</p><p>In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Fielder" title="Nathan Fielder">Nathan Fielder</a>, a Canadian comedian behind the hit show <i><a href="/wiki/Nathan_for_You" title="Nathan for You">Nathan for You</a></i>, opened a store called "<a href="/wiki/Dumb_Starbucks_Coffee" class="mw-redirect" title="Dumb Starbucks Coffee">Dumb Starbucks Coffee</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Los_Feliz" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Feliz">Los Feliz</a>, Los Angeles CA. The store resembled a typical Starbucks with one exception: everything was preceded by the word "dumb." For example, the drinks he carried included Dumb Skinny Vanilla Lattes and Dumb Frapuccinos.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> The store carried music titled "Dumb Jazz Standards" and "Dumb Norah Jones Duets."<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> He thought he could bypass infringement and copyright claims through the "Parody Law," referring to the parody aspect of <a href="/wiki/Fair_Use" class="mw-redirect" title="Fair Use">Fair Use</a> laws (that protect parodists such as <a href="/wiki/%22Weird_Al%22_Yankovic" title='"Weird Al" Yankovic'>"Weird Al" Yankovic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturday_Night_Live" title="Saturday Night Live">SNL</a>). No lawsuits were filed though because the store was short-lived. The Los Angeles Health Department shut it down after 4 days because Fielder lacked the proper permits.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup></p><p>Others have used the Starbucks logo unaltered and without permission, such as a café in Pakistan that used the logo in 2003 in its advertisements<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> and a cafe in Cambodia in 2009, the owner saying that "whatever we have done we have done within the law."<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(7)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_and_social_policies">Environmental and social policies</span></h2><section class="mf-section-7 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-7">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_Grounds_for_your_Garden.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Starbucks_Grounds_for_your_Garden.jpg/170px-Starbucks_Grounds_for_your_Garden.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="890" data-file-height="1270"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 243px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Starbucks_Grounds_for_your_Garden.jpg/170px-Starbucks_Grounds_for_your_Garden.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="243" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_Grounds_for_your_Garden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Grounds for your Garden</div></div></div>
<p>In 1999, Starbucks started "Grounds for your Garden" to make their business <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendlier" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmentally friendlier">environmentally friendlier</a>. This gives leftover coffee grounds to anyone requesting it for composting. Although not all stores and regions participate, customers can request and lobby their local store to begin the practice.
</p><p>In October 2008, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)" title="The Sun (United Kingdom)">The Sun</a></i> newspaper reported that Starbucks was wasting 23.4 million liters (6.2 million US gal) of water a day by leaving a tap constantly running for rinsing utensils in a '<a href="/wiki/Dipper_well" title="Dipper well">dipper well</a>' in each of its stores,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but this is often required by governmental <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">public health code</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup></p><p>In June 2009, in response to concerns over its excessive water consumption, Starbucks re-evaluated its use of the dipper well system. In September 2009, company-operated Starbucks stores in Canada and the United States successfully implemented a new water saving solution that meets government health standards. Different types of milk are given a dedicated spoon that remains in the pitcher and the dipper wells were replaced with push button metered faucets for rinsing. This will reportedly save up to 150 US gal (570 l) of water per day in every store.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (October 2012)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling">Recycling</span></h4>
<p>Starbucks began using 10% <a href="/wiki/Recycled_paper" class="mw-redirect" title="Recycled paper">recycled paper</a> in its beverage cups in 2006—the company claimed that the initiative was the first time that recycled material had been used in a product that came into direct contact with a food or beverage.<sup id="cite_ref-nytrecycle_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytrecycle-245">[245]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allen_Hershkowitz" title="Allen Hershkowitz">Allen Hershkowitz</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_Resources_Defense_Council" title="Natural Resources Defense Council">Natural Resources Defense Council</a> called the 10% content "minuscule,"<sup id="cite_ref-nytrecycle_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytrecycle-245">[245]</a></sup> but Starbucks received the National Recycling Coalition Recycling Works Award in 2005 for the initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup></p><p>In a 2008 media article, Starbucks's vice president of corporate social responsibility acknowledged that the company continued to struggle with environmental responsibility, as none of its cups were recyclable and stores did not have recycling bins. At the time that the article was published, Starbucks gave customers who brought in their own reusable cup a 10-cent discount, in addition to using corrugated cup sleeves made from 85 percent post-consumer recycled fiber, which is 34 percent less paper than the original. During the same period, Starbucks entered into a partnership with Conservation International—pledging US$7.5 million over three years—to help protect the natural environment of coffee-growing communities in Mexico and Indonesia.<sup id="cite_ref-seattletimes_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes-247">[247]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Farmer_equity_practices">Farmer equity practices</span></h3>
<p>Starbucks began drafting plans for <a href="/wiki/Corporate_social_responsibility" title="Corporate social responsibility">corporate social responsibility</a> in 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> Since Starbucks has partnered with <a href="/wiki/Conservation_International" title="Conservation International">Conservation International</a> (CI) to draft plans and audit its coffee and farmer equity (C.A.F.E.) program,<sup id="cite_ref-Starbucks_Coffee_Corporation_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Starbucks_Coffee_Corporation-249">[249]</a></sup> Starbucks's C.A.F.E. practices are based on a rating system of 249 indicators. Farmers who earn high overall scores receive higher prices than those who achieve lower scores. Ratings categories include economic accountability, social responsibility, environmental leadership in coffee growing and processing. Indicators for social responsibility have evolved and now include 'zero tolerance' indicators that require workers to be paid in cash, check, or direct deposit, ensure that all workers are paid the established minimum wage, that workplaces are free of harassment and abuse, that workplaces are nondiscriminatory and do not employ persons under the age of 14, and several more.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup></p><p>Starbucks has moved 90% of its coffee purchases to preferred C.A.F.E. certified providers, and the company is approaching its stated goal to purchase 100% of its coffee through C.A.F.E. or other 'ethically sourced' certification systems.<sup id="cite_ref-Starbucks_Coffee_Corporation_249-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Starbucks_Coffee_Corporation-249">[249]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/Washington_State_University" title="Washington State University">Washington State University</a> Assistant Professor Daniel Jaffee argues that Starbucks' sC.A.F.E. practices merely '<a href="/wiki/Greenwashing" title="Greenwashing">green wash</a>' "to burnish their corporate image."<sup id="cite_ref-Brewing_Justice_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brewing_Justice-251">[251]</a></sup> Additionally, Professor Marie-Christine Renard of Rural Sociology of <a href="/wiki/Chapingo_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Chapingo University">Chapingo University</a> in Mexico wrote a case study of Starbucks's, Conservation International's, and Agro-industries United of Mexico (AMSA)'s joint conservation effort in <a href="/wiki/Chiapas" title="Chiapas">Chiapas</a>, Mexico in which she concluded that "[w]hile the CI-Starbucks-AMSA Alliance paid better prices, it did not allow the producers to appropriate the knowledge that was necessary for the organizations to improve the quality of their coffee."<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Fair_trade">Fair trade</span></h3>
<p>In 2000, the company introduced a line of <a href="/wiki/Fair_trade" title="Fair trade">fair trade</a> products.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> Of the approximately 136,000 tons (300 million pounds) of coffee Starbucks purchased in 2006, about 18 million pounds or 6% was certified as fair trade.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup></p><p>According to Starbucks, they purchased 2,180 metric tons (4.8 million pounds) of Certified Fair Trade coffee in fiscal year 2004 and 5,220 metric tons (11.5 million pounds) in 2005. As of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Starbucks&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> they were the largest buyer of Certified Fair Trade coffee in North America (10% of the global market). <a href="/wiki/Transfair_USA" class="mw-redirect" title="Transfair USA">Transfair USA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (June 2013)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>All espresso roast sold in the UK and Ireland is <a href="/wiki/Fairtrade" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairtrade">Fairtrade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> Questions have been raised regarding the legitimacy of the Fair Trade designation.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup></p><p>Groups such as <a href="/wiki/Global_Exchange" title="Global Exchange">Global Exchange</a> are calling for Starbucks to further increase its sales of fair trade coffees.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup></p><p>According to Starbucks, in 2004 it paid on average $1.42 per pound ($2.64 kg) for high-quality coffee beans, 74% above the commodity prices at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup></p><p>After a long-running dispute between Starbucks and Ethiopia, Starbucks agreed to support and promote Ethiopian coffees. An article in BBC News,<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> states that Ethiopian ownership of popular coffee designations such as Harrar and Sidamo is acknowledged, even if they are not registered. Ethiopia fought hard for this acknowledgement mainly to help give its poverty-stricken farmers a chance to make more money. In 2006, Starbucks said it paid $1.42 per pound for its coffee. The coffee Starbucks bought for $1.42 per pound, had a selling price—after transportation, processing, marketing, store rentals, taxes, and staff salary and benefits—of $10.99 per pound.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> As of August 2010, the Starbucks website sells only one Ethiopian coffee, which it says is new.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (November 2019)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>In addition, Starbucks is an active member of the <a href="/wiki/World_Cocoa_Foundation" title="World Cocoa Foundation">World Cocoa Foundation</a>.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethos_water">Ethos water</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ethos_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethos water">Ethos</a>, a brand of bottled water acquired by Starbucks in 2003, is sold at locations throughout North America. Ethos bottles feature prominent labeling stating "helping children get clean water," referring to the fact that US$0.05 from each US$1.80 bottle sold (US$0.10 per bottle in Canada) is used to fund clean water projects in under-developed areas. Although sales of Ethos water have raised over US$6.2 million for clean water efforts, the brand is not incorporated as a charity. Critics have argued that the claim on the label misleads consumers into thinking that Ethos is primarily a charitable organization when it is actually a for-profit brand and the vast majority of the sale price (97.2%) does not support clean-water projects.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup></p><p>The founders of Ethos have stated that the brand is intended to raise awareness of third-world clean water issues and provide socially responsible consumers with an opportunity to support the cause by choosing Ethos over other brands.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> Starbucks has since redesigned the American version of the Ethos water bottles, stating the amount of money donated per bottle in the description.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_bank_donations">Food bank donations</span></h3>
<p>Since 2010, Starbucks has been donating leftover pastries in the United States to local food banks through a food collection service named Food Donation Connection.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2016, Starbucks unveiled a five-year plan to donate 100 percent of unsold food from its 7,600 company-operated stores in the U.S. to local food banks and pantries.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> Perishable food will be transported in refrigerated trucks to area food banks through the company's partnerships with the <a href="/wiki/Food_Donation_Connection" title="Food Donation Connection">Food Donation Connection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feeding_America" title="Feeding America">Feeding America</a>. This program, called FoodShare, is expected to provide up to 50 million meals over the next five years.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup></p><p>As of 2017, the program was in 10 different markets, including New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup> In New York, Starbucks works with Feeding America and City Harvest, both non-profits, to donate food from 45 locations. It plans to expand the program to all 305 Manhattan stores.
</p><p>In September 2019, 60% of Starbucks stores are participating in FoodShare. This level of participation contributed to 20 million meals served to those in need. <sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2020_plastic_straw_ban">2020 plastic straw ban</span></h3>
<p>On July 9, 2018, the Starbucks President and CEO, <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Johnson_(executive)" title="Kevin Johnson (executive)">Kevin Johnson (executive)</a> announced that Starbucks will ban the single-use <a href="/wiki/Plastic_straw" class="mw-redirect" title="Plastic straw">plastic straws</a> by 2020 on all cold drinks from all locations worldwide due to climate change concerns, pollution, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtle</a> endangerment as the single-use plastic straws failed to be designed for recycling when they were invented. The <a href="/wiki/Frappucino" class="mw-redirect" title="Frappucino">frappucinos</a> will get straws made from a different material that is sustainable and environmentally friendly such as paper or compostable plastic, while other cold drinks will get straw-less lids. However, the new lids will actually have more plastic than the old lid-straw combination but they are recyclable unlike the old lid-single-use straw combination.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup> The Starbucks locations in Europe, China, Taiwan, Japan, Thailand, Vietnam, India, Canada, Hawaii, Alaska, Washington D.C., New Mexico, California, New York, Washington State, New Jersey, Oregon, Maryland, Delaware, Vermont, Massachusetts, Connecticut, and Rhode Island already took effect of the change/plastic straw ban.<sup id="cite_ref-market_down_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-market_down-271">[271]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fortune.com_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fortune.com-272">[272]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_Coffeeshops_Closings_Shankbone_2012.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Starbucks_Coffeeshops_Closings_Shankbone_2012.jpg/220px-Starbucks_Coffeeshops_Closings_Shankbone_2012.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1936"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 164px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Starbucks_Coffeeshops_Closings_Shankbone_2012.jpg/220px-Starbucks_Coffeeshops_Closings_Shankbone_2012.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="164" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_Coffeeshops_Closings_Shankbone_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A local coffee shop in New York's <a href="/wiki/East_Village_(Manhattan)" class="mw-redirect" title="East Village (Manhattan)">East Village</a> claiming it had to close because Starbucks is willing to pay higher rent for the space</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Starbucks" title="Criticism of Starbucks">Criticism of Starbucks</a></div>
<p>Starbucks has been accused of selling unhealthy products, and has been the subject of multiple controversies.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel">Israel</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_boarded_up_after_riot.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Starbucks_boarded_up_after_riot.jpg/220px-Starbucks_boarded_up_after_riot.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Starbucks_boarded_up_after_riot.jpg/220px-Starbucks_boarded_up_after_riot.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Starbucks_boarded_up_after_riot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A store on <a href="/wiki/Piccadilly" title="Piccadilly">Piccadilly</a> with its windows boarded up after being smashed by protesters after a <a href="/wiki/Stop_the_War_Coalition" title="Stop the War Coalition">Stop the War Coalition</a> rally in 2009</div></div></div>
<p>Born into a <a href="/wiki/American_Jews" title="American Jews">Jewish</a> family, <a href="/wiki/Howard_Schultz" title="Howard Schultz">Howard Schultz</a> "has kept his Jewish life private" according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Forward" title="The Forward">The Forward</a></i>, while "support[ing] a range of Jewish spiritual and humanitarian causes."<sup id="cite_ref-forward_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forward-275">[275]</a></sup> He has been criticized for criticizing <a href="/wiki/Yasser_Arafat" title="Yasser Arafat">Yasser Arafat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Svetlova_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svetlova-276">[276]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> In 2004, Starbucks ended its operations in Israel, stating that this was due to market challenges, not political reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-starbucks3_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starbucks3-278">[278]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-forward_275-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forward-275">[275]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Snopes2_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snopes2-279">[279]</a></sup></p><p>Since 2006, there have been perennial calls to boycott Starbucks based on false claims that the company and Schultz provided financial support to the Israeli government and <a href="/wiki/Israeli_military" class="mw-redirect" title="Israeli military">military</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-forward_275-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forward-275">[275]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jta_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jta-280">[280]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> The claims have been traced to a piece of satire written by Australian weblogger Andrew Winkler.<sup id="cite_ref-Snopes_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snopes-282">[282]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Svetlova_276-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svetlova-276">[276]</a></sup> Starbucks stores in both London and <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a> have been attacked by pro-<a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestinian</a> protesters as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-beirut_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beirut-283">[283]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wecouldnt_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wecouldnt-284">[284]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Tax_avoidance">Tax avoidance</span></h3>
<p>In October 2012, Starbucks faced criticism after a <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> investigation found that the company reportedly paid only £8.6 million in <a href="/wiki/Corporation_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporation tax">corporation tax</a> in the UK over 14 years, despite generating over £3 billion in sales—this included no tax payments on £1.3 billion of sales in the three years prior to 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-birkin1610_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birkin1610-288">[288]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup> It is alleged that Starbucks was able to do this by charging high licensing fees to the UK branch of the business, allowing them to declare a £33 million loss in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup> The UK subsidiary pays <a href="/wiki/Patent_fee" class="mw-redirect" title="Patent fee">patent fees</a> to the US subsidiary, purchases coffee beans from the Netherlands subsidiary (where <a href="/wiki/Corporate_tax_in_the_Netherlands#Tax_haven" title="Corporate tax in the Netherlands">corporation tax is lower than in the UK</a>), and uses the Swiss subsidiary for other "miscellaneous services."<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/YouGov" title="YouGov">YouGov</a> survey suggested that Starbucks's brand image was substantially weakened by the controversy surrounding how much tax it pays in the UK several weeks after the allegations surfaced.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Philadelphia_arrests">Philadelphia arrests</span></h3>
<p>On April 12, 2018, two African-American men, Donte Robinson and Rashon Nelson, were arrested in a Starbucks store in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wfmynews2.com_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wfmynews2.com-293">[293]</a></sup> A witness at the time of the arrests said that the men asked the staff if they could use the bathroom to which an employee said it was reserved for paying customers. The men waited at a table for another person to join them, without ordering, and were instructed by the staff to either make a purchase or leave. When they didn't comply, the store manager called the police, saying the men were <a href="/wiki/Trespassing" class="mw-redirect" title="Trespassing">trespassing</a>, which led to the arrests. They were later released without charges being pressed.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup> The video of the arrest went viral and prompted the CEO of Starbucks <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Johnson_(executive)" title="Kevin Johnson (executive)">Kevin Johnson</a> to issue an apology. On ABC's "<a href="/wiki/Good_Morning_America" title="Good Morning America">Good Morning America</a>," Johnson appeared for an interview and expressed his desire to meet with the men in person to apologize. He referred to the arrests as "reprehensible," and promised to take steps to prevent future incidents.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup></p><p>The company subsequently announced it would host a nationwide anti-bias training for approximately 175,000 U.S. employees.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2018, the company and the City of Philadelphia announced settlement agreements with Robinson and Nelson. The men received a token settlement of $1 each from the city, while the city agreed to provide $200,000 in funding for a young entrepreneurs program for public school students. Starbucks agreed to an undisclosed financial settlement with the two men, and will also provide them with free tuition toward a bachelor's degree through the Starbucks College Achievement Plan, a joint initiative with <a href="/wiki/Arizona_State_University" title="Arizona State University">Arizona State University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Alleged_transgender_discrimination">Alleged transgender discrimination</span></h3>
<p>In July 2018 a transgender former employee of a Starbucks in Fresno, CA sued the company for discrimination and harassment.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup> The lawsuit claims the employee, Maddie Wade, was harassed and discriminated against after disclosing to her manager that she had started the process to transition from male to female.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup></p><p>According to the lawsuit, Wade began working for Starbucks in 2008 and in October 2017, was diagnosed with gender dysphoria. The suit alleges that Wade disclosed her gender dysphoria diagnosis to her manager who proceeded to reduce her hours, ultimately transferring to another location before resigning.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup> The complaint includes various screenshots from some of the manager's social media posts.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-306">[306]</a></sup> In a statement concerning the suit, Starbucks said: "At Starbucks, we strive to create a culture of warmth and belonging, where everyone is welcome and have zero tolerance for the harassment of our partners or customers."<sup id="cite_ref-:5_306-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-306">[306]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(9)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span id="Music.2C_film.2C_and_television"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Music,_film,_and_television">Music, film, and television</span></h2><section class="mf-section-9 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-9">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hear_Music" title="Hear Music">Hear Music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hearmusic_riverwalk.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hearmusic_riverwalk.jpg/220px-Hearmusic_riverwalk.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="370" data-file-height="320"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 190px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Hearmusic_riverwalk.jpg/220px-Hearmusic_riverwalk.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="190" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hearmusic_riverwalk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Starbucks's second Hear Music Coffeehouse at the South Bank development adjacent to the <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio_River_Walk" title="San Antonio River Walk">River Walk</a> in downtown <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio,_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="San Antonio, Texas">San Antonio, Texas</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Hear Music began as a music catalog company in 1990, adding a few retail locations in the San Francisco Bay Area. Hear Music was purchased by Starbucks in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup> In 2002, they produced a Starbucks opera album, featuring artists such as <a href="/wiki/Luciano_Pavarotti" title="Luciano Pavarotti">Luciano Pavarotti</a>, followed in March 2007 by the hit CD "<a href="/wiki/Memory_Almost_Full" title="Memory Almost Full">Memory Almost Full</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a>, making McCartney the first artist signed to the new Hear Music label sold in Starbucks outlets.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup></p><p>In 2006, the company created Starbucks Entertainment, one of the producers of the 2006 film <i><a href="/wiki/Akeelah_and_the_Bee" title="Akeelah and the Bee">Akeelah and the Bee</a></i>. Retail stores advertised the film before its release and sold the DVD.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup></p><p>Starbucks has become the subject of a protest song, "<a href="/wiki/A_Rock_Star_Bucks_a_Coffee_Shop" title="A Rock Star Bucks a Coffee Shop">A Rock Star Bucks a Coffee Shop</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Neil_Young" title="Neil Young">Neil Young</a> and his band, <a href="/wiki/Promise_of_the_Real" class="mw-redirect" title="Promise of the Real">Promise of the Real</a>. This single from the album <i><a href="/wiki/The_Monsanto_Years" title="The Monsanto Years">The Monsanto Years</a></i> criticized both Starbucks's alleged use of <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified food</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">GMO</a> company <a href="/wiki/Monsanto" title="Monsanto">Monsanto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup> On May 31, 2015, the song was Video of the week on FoodConsumer.org.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(10)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2><section class="mf-section-10 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-10">
